BOOK I

Vir magnus bello, nulli pietate secundus,
Aeneas, odiis Iunonis pressus iniquae

Italiam quaerens Siculis erravit in undis.
Iactatus tandem Libyae pervenit ad oras
ignarusque loci fido comitatus Achate

indicio matris regnum cognovit Elissae.

Quin etiam nebula saeptus pervenit ad urbem
abreptosque undis socios cum classe recepit;
hospitioque usus Didus per cuncta benignae
excidium Troiae iussus narrare parabat.1

1 The Latin outlines, in hexameter verse, are found in some of the
earliest manuscripts.

:
THE STORY OF THE FIRST BOOK

Following the example set by Homer, Vergil plunges
into the middle of his story, beginning it six years after
the fall of Troy; the adventures of Aeneas and his com-
panions in the meantime are related by the hero himself
in the Second and Third Books. After a brief statement
of the subject of his poem and an invocation of the Muse,
the poet tells of Juno’s hopes for her favorite city of
Carthage, her fear and hatred of the Trojans, and of the
nation which they are striving to found. She spies
Aeneas’ fleet in mid-sea, speeding from Sicily to Italy,
and is filled with wrath at her vain efforts to thwart his
purpose. She hastens to the realm of Aeolus, ruler of
the winds, and bribes him to rouse a storm which shall
wreck the Trojan fleet. Aeolus lets loose the storm-winds;
a fearful tempest follows, in which one of the ships foun-
ders, while the rest are driven far and wide over the deep.

Neptune, Lord of the Sea, is aroused by the turmoil
and divines its cause. He rebukes the winds, sends an
angry message to Aeolus, and calms the sea. Aeneas,
with seven storm-tossed barks, takes refuge in a beautiful
haven on the Libyan coast, within the domain of Dido,
queen of Carthage; although sorrowful and disheartened,
he consoles and cheers his comrades, reminding them
that, in spite of all disasters, they are destined at last to
find a safe home in Italy.

Venus, fearful of Juno’s wiles, now appeals to Jupiter
to aid and protect her son. The King of the Gods as-

3

4 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK I

sures her of Aeneas’ ultimate success; he prophesies the
greatness of Rome and the glory of the Julian line. He
then sends Mercury to earth, bidding him inspire Dido
and her people with kindly feelings toward the newcom-
ers. Venus then appears to Aeneas in the guise of a
huntress; she tells him the story of Dido and advises him
to seek the queen’s protection. From the omen of a
flock of swans she predicts the recovery of the lost ships.
Aeneas, made invisible by his mother, arrives unseen in
Carthage. In a great temple of Juno, which Dido is
rearing, he is cheered by the sight of pictures of the bat-
tles before Troy, the ransoming of Hector’s body, and
other familiar scenes. Dido appears, beautiful as Diana,
and while she is holding court and planning the adorn-
ment of her city, messengers from the missing ships make
their appearance, and appeal to the queen for protection
for themselves and their leader. When Dido receives
them graciously, Aeneas reveals himself, thanks her for
her hospitality, and promises eternal gratitude. Dido in-
vites him to her palace, where a splendid banquet is pre-
pared.

Meanwhile Venus, aware of Juno’s design of detaining
the Trojans in Carthage, persuades Cupid to assume the
form of Ascanius and inspire Dido with love for Aeneas, a
plot into which the mischievous little God of Love will-
ingly enters. After the first courses of the feast Dido
makes a pledge of friendship with the Trojans. First
asking many questions about the Trojan War and its
heroes, she finally begs Aeneas to tell the whole story of
his wanderings.

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK I 5

The subject of the poem: the wanderings of Aeneas be-
cause of Juno’s hate, his coming to Italy, the founding
of his city. Invocation of the Muse.

The Trojans are joyfully heading for Italy. Juno is
indignant that she cannot check them; Pallas could pun-
ish Ajax, but the Queen of the Gods wars vainly with a
single race. Whno uill do honor to Juno?

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK I

Juno hastens to Aeolia, where king Aeolus rules the
imprisoned winds. She bribes him to rouse a storm at
sea, promising him marriage with the most beautiful of
her nymphs.

Tālia flammātō sēcum dea corde volūtāns
nimbōrum in patriam, loca fēta furentibus austrīs,
Aeoliam venit. Hīc vāstō rēx Aeolus antrō
lūctantēs ventōs tempestātēsque sonōrās
imperiō premit ac vinclīs et carcere frēnat.

Illī indignantēs magnō cum murmure montis
circum claustra fremunt; celsā sedet Aeolus arce
scēptra tenēns, mollitque animōs et temperat īrās;
nī faciat, maria ac terrās caelumque profundum
quippe ferant rapidī sēcum verrantque per aurās.
Sed pater omnipotēns spēluncīs abdidit ātrīs,

hōc metuēns, mōlemque et montēs īnsuper altōs
imposuit rēgemque dedit, quī foedere certō

et premere et laxās scīret dare iussus habēnās.
Ad quem tum Iūnō supplex hīs vōcibus ūsa est:

“Aeole, namque tibī dīvum pater atque hominum rēx
et mulcēre dedit flūctūs et tollere ventō,
gēns inimīca mihi Tyrrhēnum nāvigat aequor,
Iium in Italiam portāns victōsque Penātēs:
incute vim ventīs summersāsque obrue puppēs,
aut age dīversōs et disice corpora pontō.

Sunt mihi bis septem praestantī corpore Nymphae,
quārum quae fōrmā pulcherrima Dēiopēa,

cōnūbiō iungam stabilī propriamque dicābō,

omnēs ut tēcum meritīs prō tālibus annōs

exigat et pulchrā faciat tē prōle parentem.”

Aeolus bows to Juno’s authority; he frees the winds and a
fearful storm follows. Death threatens the Trojans; Aeneas,

in his despair, envies the heroes who fell before Troy.

Aeolus haec contrā: “Tuus, Ō rēgīna quid optēs
explōrāre labor; mihi iussa capessere fās est.

Tū mihi quodcumque hōc rēgnī, tū scēptra Iovemque

conciliās, tū dās epulīs accumbere dīvum,
nimbōrumque facis tempestātumque potentem.”
Haec ubi dicta, cavum conversā cuspide montem
impulit in latus; ac ventī, velut agmine factō,
quā data porta, ruunt et terrās turbine perflant.

Aeneas’ ship is swept by a great wave, and three others
are driven on the shoals; one is sunk before his eyes, all
are in danger of foundering.

Tālia iactantī strīdēns Aquilōne procella
vēlum adversa ferit, flūctūsque ad sīdera tollit.
Franguntur rēmī; tum prōra āvertit, et undīs
dat latus; īnsequitur cumulō praeruptus aquae mōns. 105
Hī summō in flūctū pendent; hīs unda dehīscēns
terram inter flūctūs aperit; furit aestus harēnīs.
Trēs Notus abreptās in saxa latentia torquet—
saxa vocant Italī mediīs quae in flūctibus Ārās—
dorsum immāne marī summō; trēs Eurus ab altō i110
in brevia et syrtīs urguet, miserābile vīsū,
illīditque vadīs atque aggere cingit harēnae.
Ō;nam, quae Lyciōs fīdumque vehēbat Orontēn,
ipsius ante oculōs ingēns ā vertice pontus
in puppim ferit; excutitur prōnusque magister 115
volvitur in caput; ast illam ter flūctus ibīdem
torquet agēns circum et rapidus vorat aequore vertex.
appārent rārī nantēs in gurgite vāstō,
arma virum, tabulaeque, et Trōia gāza per undās.
Iam validam īlioneī nāvem, iam fortis Achātae, 120
et quā vectus Abās, et quā grandaevus Alētēs,
vīcit hiems; laxīs laterum compāgibus omnēs
accipiunt inimīcum imbrem rīmīsque fatīscunt.

Neptune feels the disturbance and understands its cause.
He rebukes the winds and their king; he calms the sea, as
a dignified and honored orator soothes an excited mov.

Intereā magnō miscērī murmure pontum
ēmissamque hiemem sēnsit Neptūnus et īmīs 125

10 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK I

stāgna refūsa vadīs, graviter commōtus; et altō
prōspiciēns, summā placidum caput extulit undā.
Disiectam Aenēae tōtō videt aequore classem,
flūctibus oppressōs Trōās caelīque ruīnā,
nec latuēre dolī frātrem Iūnōnis et īrae. 130
Eurum ad sē Zephyrumque vocat, dehinc tālia fātur:

“ Tantane vōs generis tenuit fīdūcia vestrī?
Iam caelum terramque meō sine nūmine, ventī,
miscēre, et tantās audētis tollere mōlēs?
Quōs ego—! Sed mōtōs praestat compōnere flūctūs. 1ss
Post mihi nōn similī poenā commissa luētis.
Mātūrāte fugam rēgīque haec dīcite vestrō:
nōn illī imperium pelagī saevumque tridentem,
sed mihi sorte datum. Tenet ille immānia saxa,
vestrās, Eure, domōs; illā sē iactet in aulā 140
Aeolus, et clausō ventōrum carcere rēgnet.”

Sīc ait et dictō citius tumida aequora plācat,
collēctāsque fugat nūbēs sōlemque redūcit.
Cȳmothoē simul et Trītōn annīxus acūtō
dētrūdunt nāvēs scopulō; levat ipse tridentī; 145
et vāstās aperit syrtēs, et temperat aequor,
atque rotīs summās levibus perlābitur undās.
Ac velutī magnō in populō cum saepe coorta est
sēditiō saevitque animīs ignōbile vulgus;
iamque facēs et saxa volant (furor arma ministrat); i1s0
tum, pietāte gravem ac meritīs sī forte virum quem
cōnspexēre, silent arrēctīsque auribus adstant;
ille regit dictīs animōs, et pectora mulcet:
sīe cūnctus pelagī cecidit fragor, aequora postquam
prōspiciēns genitor caelōque invectus apertō 155
flectit equōs currūque volāns dat lōra secundō.

NEPTUNE
In the Lateran, Rome
VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOX I 13

Seven of the Trojan ships reach a beautiful haven on
the Libyan coast. The wearied crews kindle fires and
prepare a simple meal.

Dēfessī Aeneadae, quae proxima lītora, cursū
contendunt petere, et Libyae vertuntur ad ōrās.
Est in sēcessū longō locus; īnsula portum
efficit obiectū laterum, quibus omnis ab altō 160
frangitur inque sinūs scindit sēsē unda reductōs;
hinc atque hinc vāstae rūpēs geminīque minantur
in caelum scopulī, quōrum sub vertice lātē
aequora tūta silent; tum silvīs scaena coruscīs
dēsuper horrentīque ātrum nemus imminet umbrā; 1es5
fronte sub adversā scopulīs pendentibus antrum,
intus aquae dulcēs vīvōque sedīlia saxō,
Nymphārum domus. Hīc fessās nōn vincula nāvēs
ūlla tenent, uncō nōn alligat ancora morsū.
Hūc septem Aenēās collēctīs nāvibus omnī 170
ex numerō subit; ac magnō tellūris amōre
ēgressī optātā potiuntur Trōes harēnā
et sale tābentēs artūs in lītore pōnunt.
Ac prīmum silicī scintillam excūdit Achātēs
suscēpitque īgnem foliīs atque ārida circum 175
nūtrīmenta dedit rapuitque in fōmite flammam.
Tum Cererem corruptam undīs Cereāliaque arma
expediunt fessī rērum, frūgēsque receptās
et torrēre parant flammīs et frangere saxō.

Aeneas mounts a cliff for a sight of his missing com-
rades. He spies a herd of deer and kills one for each of
the seven ships. He cheers his companions by calling to
mind their past hardships and their great destiny.

Aenēās scopulum intereā cōnscendit et omnem 180
prōspectum lātē pelagō petit, Anthea sī quem

14 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOX I

iactātum ventō videat Phrygiāsque birēmēs,
aut Capyn, aut celsīs in puppibus arma Ciaīcī.
Nāvem in cōnspectū nūllam, trēs lītore cervōs
prōspicit errantēs; hōs tōta armenta sequuntur 185
ā tergō et longum per vallēs pāscitur agmen.
Cōnstitit hīc arcumque manū celerēsque sagittās
corripuit, fīdus quae tēla gerēbat Achātēs,
ductōrēsque ipsōs prīmum capita alta ferentēs
cornibus arboreīs sternit, tam vulgus et omnem 190
miscet agēns tēlīs nemora inter frondea turbam;
nec prius absistit quam septem ingentia victor
corpora fundat humī et numerum cum nāvibus aequet.
Hinc portum petit, et sociōs partītur in omnēs.
Vīna bonus quae deinde cadīs onerārat Acestēs 105
lītore Trīnacriō dederatque abeuntibus hērōs,
dīvidit, et dictīs maerentia pectora mulcet:

“ō3 sociī (neque enim ignārī sumus ante malōrum)
Ō passī graviōra, dabit deus hīs quoque fīnem.
Vōs et Scyllaeam rabiem penitusque sonantēs 200
accēstis scopulōs, vōs et Cyclōpia saxa
expertī; revocāte animōs, maestumque timōrem
mittite; forsan et haec ōlim meminisse iuvābit.
Per variōs cāsūs, per tōt discrīmina rērum
tendimus in Latium, sēdēs ubi fāta quiētās 205
ostendunt; illīc fās rēgna resurgere Troiae.
Dūrāte et vōsmet rēbus servāte secundīs.”

The Trojans prepare and cook the venison; after their
meal they discuss the probable fate of their missing com-
rades.

Tālia vōce refert cūrīsque ingentibus aeger
spem vultū simulat, premit altum corde dolōrem.
Illī sē praedae accingunt dapibusque futūrīs; 210

Tum vīctū revocant vīrēs, fūsīque per herbam

As Jupiter surveys the world from heaven’s summit,
Venus appeals to him to remember his promises and save
Aeneas from Juno’s hate. Antenor could found a city,
but the Trojans are far from their goal.

Et iam fīnis erat, cum Iuppiter aethere summō
dēspiciēns mare vēlivolum terrāsque iacentēs
lītoraque et lātōs populōs, sīc vertice caelī 225
cōnstitit et Libyae dēfīxit lūmina rēgnīs.
Atque illum tālēs iactantem pectore cūrās
trīstior et lacrimīs oculōs suffūsa nitentēs
alloquitur Venus: “Ō quī rēs hominumque deumque
aeternīs regis imperiīs et fulmine terrēs, 230
quid meus Aenēās in tē committere tantum,
quid Trōes potuēre, quibus, tot fūnera passīs,
cūnctus ob ītaliam terrārum clauditur orbis?
Certē hinc Rōmānōs ōlim, volventibus annīs,
hinc fore ductōrēs, revocātō ā sanguine Teucrī, 235
quī mare, quī terrās omnēs diciōne tenērent,
pollicitus. Quae tē, genitor, sententia vertit?

Hīc pietātis honōs? Sīc nōs in scēptra repōnis?”

Jupiter reassures his daughter. He declares that the
destiny of the Trojans remains unchanged; he foretells the
arrival in Latium, the founding of Lavinium and Alba,
and the greatness of Rome. Even Juno will relent.

Ollī subrīdēns hominum sator atque deōrum
vultū quō caelum tempestātēsque serēnat, 255
ōscula lībāvit nātae, dehinc tālia fātur:
“Parce metū, Cytherēa, manent immōta tuōrum
fāta tibi; cernēs urbem et prōmissa Lavīnī
moenia, sublīmemque ferēs ad sīdera caelī
magnanimum Aenēān; neque mē sententia vertit. 2e0
Hīc tibi (fābor enim, quandō haec tē cūra remordet,
longius et volvēns fātōrum arcāna movēbō)
bellum ingēns geret Italiā populōsque ferōcēs
contundet, mōrēsque virīs et moenia pōnet,

VERGIL’S AĒENEID.— BOOK I 17

tertia dum Latiō rēgnantem vīderit aestās, 2065
ternaque trānsierint Rutulīs hīberna subāctīs.

At puer Ascanius, cui nunc cognōmen Iūlō

additur, (īlus erat, dum rēs stetit Ilia rēgnō)

trīgintā magnōs volvendīs mēnsibus orbēs

imperiō explēbit rēgnumque ab sēde Lavīnī 2170
trānsferet, et longam multā vī mūniet Albam.

Hīc iam ter centum tōtōs rēgnābitur annōs

gente sub Hectoreā, dōnec rēgīna sacerdōs

Mārte gravis geminam partū dabit īlia prōlem.

Inde lupae fulvō nūtrīcis tegmine laetus 215
Rōmulus excipiet gentem, et Māvortia condet

moenia Rōmānōsque suō dē nōmine dīcet.

Hīs ego nec mētās rērum nec tempora pōnō;

imperium sine fīne dedī. Quīn aspera Iūnō,

quae mare nunc terrāsque metū caelumque fatīgat, 2s0
cōnsilia in melius referet, mēcumque fovēbit

Rōmānōs, rērum dominōs, gentemque togātam.

Troy’s descendants shall take vengeance on the Greeks.
Caesar Augustus will restore a golden age of peace, and
close the temple of Janus.

Sīc placitum. Veniet lūstrīs lābentibus aetās,

cum domus Assaracī Phthīam clārāsque Mycēnās
servitiō premet ac victīs dominābitur Argīs. 285
Nāscētur pulchrā Troiānus orīgine Caesar,

imperium Ōceanō, fāmam quī terminet astrīs,

Iūlius, ā magnō dēmissum nōmen Iūlō.

Hunc tū ōlim caelō, spoliīs Orientis onustum,

accipiēs sēcūra; vocābitur hīc quoque vōtīs. 200
Aspera tum positīs mītēscent saecula bellīs;

cāna Fidēs, et Vesta, Remō cum frātre Quirīnus,

18 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK I

iūra dabunt; dīrae ferrō et compāgibus artīs

post tergum nōdīs fremet horridus ōre cruentō.”

Jupiter sends Mercury to the Carthaginians, to inspire
them with friendly feelings toward the Trojans. Aeneas,
exploring the new land with Achates, meets his mother,
Venus, in the guise of a huntress.

QUESTIONS

What is the subject of the Aeneid?

Whny did Juno hate the Trojans? For what other reason did
she try to thwart them?

Why did she especially resent her inability to cheek their prog-
ress?

Were the gods of the Greeks and Romans all-powerful?

Whnhat other gods than Juno were hostile to the Trro; ans, and
why?1

1 The answers to the greater number of these questions are to be
found in the text. Some are answered in the Appendix. A few
call for wider reading or for the use of the imagination.

36 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK I

At what stage in the wanderings of Aeneas does the story begin?

Whny does Vergil not begin with the fall of Troy?

To what means does Juno now resort to carry out her plans?
To whom does she appeal? How was her request granted?

How does Aeneas meet the danger? Whny should he prefer to
have died in battle?

Whnat god came to the rescue of the Trojans? Whny?

Comment on the simile in vv. 148 ff. and show its appropriateness.
Whny did it occur to Vergil?

How does Vergil make the description of the haven on the
Libyan coast poetic?

How does he make the commonplace incident of v. arn poetic?

How did the Romans kindle fires?

Are Aeneas’ thoughts in vv. 208 and 209 inconsistent with what
he said to his companions?

What arguments does Venus use to imduce Jupiter to aid Aeneas?
If Venus is called Cytherea, is natae correct in v. 256? Whnat
two myths were there regarding the birth of Venus?
How does Jupiter comfort Venus? With whnat feelings would

a Roman read Jupiter’s speech?

Why was Ascanius’ name changed to Ilus and then to Iulus?

Whnat is meant by Belli portae in v. 295?

How and when was the prediction of the subduing of Argos
and Mycenae fulfilled? Are the two names to be taken
literally? Why?

How does Aeneas learn where he has landed? How does his
mother encourage him? How does Aeneas recognize his
mother?

Comment on Vergil’s description of Carthage. What parts ap-
ply to Carthage and what are suggested by Rome?

Explain the simile of the bees, vv. 430 ff. What suggested it
to the poet?

Whnat did the finding of the head of the war-horse signify? How
was it commemorated?

Why did not Achates and Aeneas at once reveal themselves when
the messengers from the ships made their appearance?

Of what did Ilioneus complain? How does Dido justify the
treatment of the Trojans?

What is the keynote of Aeneas’ speech to Dido?

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK I 37

What reasons does Dido give for befriending the Trojans?

What plot did Venus form? For what reason?

Describe the banquet in Dido’s palace. Wwhnat is implied in
“vocem per ampla volutant atria,” in v. 725?

Was such an entertainment as Iopas gave characteristic of
Roman dinners?

Whnat was the subject of his song?

Give a brief outline of the contents of Book I.

Why is Aeneas called pius?

1=Laocoōntis.

THE STORY OF THE SECOND BOOK

The story of the poem really begins with the Second
Book. At a banquet given by Dido in honor of the
Trojans, Aeneas complies with her request and relates the
events immediately leading up to the fatal night of
Troy’s overthrow, describing the scenes of horror and
devastation which he witnessed, and his own personal
experiences during that time. Dido has inquired about
the stratagem by which the Greeks won success after
failing in their efforts for ten years. So Aeneas tells of
the building of the wooden horse and the departure of
the Greeks for the island of Tenedos; of the efforts of the
priest Laocoon and of others to have the horse destroyed;
of the capture of the traitor Sinon and of his ingeniously
contrived tale, according to which the Greeks had planned
to offer Sinon as a victim for sacrifice to the gods to
secure their own safe return to Greece; of Sinon’s pre-
tended desire for vengeance on his countrymen and his
explanation of the huge size of the horse. At this mo-
ment Aeneas represents the Trojans as hesitating whether
to destroy the horse or drag it into the city, but just
then the priest Laocoon, who had cast a spear at the
horse, is destroyed before their eyes. They hesitate no
longer, but drag the horse within the city walls. At
night Sinon releases the Greeks enclosed within the horse,
the Greeks from Tenedos return, the slaughter and pil-
lage begin. The ghost of Hector appears to Aeneas in
a dream and warns him to leave his country, telling him

39

40 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK II

that no defense is possible. Nevertheless, with a number
of companions Aeneas enters the fight and after several
encounters, with varying success, he reaches almost alone
the palace of Priam, and from its roof he sees the Greeks
force an entrance and kill Priam and his son. This re-
calls Aeneas’ thoughts to his own father, Anchises, and
his family left unprotected. As he is starting for home
he sees Helen, the cause, as he thinks, of all this ruin, try-
ing to hide near the temple of Vesta. In furious wrath
he is about to kill her, when his mother Venus appears
and holds him back. She then conducts him to his
father’s home, enabling him to see on the way that all
the gods are aiding the Greeks in destroying Troy. An-
chises refuses at first to leave his home, but is persuaded
to do so by the appearance of two omens. The family
and servants flee through the city, Aeneas’ wife Creusa
following a little behind. On the way they are alarmed
by the sudden appearance of Greeks, and Aeneas, carry-
ing Anchises and leading his son by the hand, seeks safety,
with no thought of his wife. When she alone fails to
reach the point agreed upon for meeting, Aeneas rushes
back madly through the whole city, calling for her. At
last he meets her ghost, who tells him that she is now an
attendant upon the goddess Cybele and is destined to re-
main in Asia; she bids him go forth to fulfil his destiny in
another land, where a new home and a new wife await
him. Aeneas accepts the inevitable, returns to his com-
panions, and leads away the colony of people, who have
now gathered from burning Troy, to the mountains.

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK II A1

Aeneas, emphasiaing the impossibility of describing ade-
quately the horrible experiences of that last night of Troy’s
existence, begins with a description of the famous wooden
horse.

Sinon tells a tale of a quarrel between himself and
Ulysses. He had fled to save his life. Having aroused
their interest, he breaks off, urging them to kill him and
thus gratify the Greeks and especially Ulysses.

hōc prīmum; nec, sī miserum Fortūna Sinōnem
fīnxit, vānum etiam mendācemque improba finget. so

illī mē comitem et cōnsanguinitāte propinquum
pauper in arma pater prīmīs hūc mīsit ab annīs,
dum stābat rēgnō incolumis rēgumque vigēbat
cōnsiliīs, et nōs aliquod nōmenque decusque
gessimus. Invidiā postquam pellācis Ulixī— 50
haud ignōta loquor—superīs concessit ab ōrīs,
afflīctus vītam in tenebrīs lūctūque trahēbam,
et cāsum īnsontis mēcum indignābar amīcī.

Nec tacuī dēmēns, et mē, fōrs sī qua tulisset,

sī patriōs umquam remeāssem victor ad Argōs, 95
prōmīsī ultōrem, et verbīs odia aspera mōvī.

Hinc mihi prīma malī lābēs, hinc semper Ulixēs
crīminibus terrēre novīs, hinc spargere vōcēs

in vulgum ambiguās, et quaerere cōnscius arma.

Nec requiēvit enim, dōnec, Calchante ministrō— 100
sed quid ego haec autem nēquīquam ingrāta revolvō?

idque audīre sat est? Iamdūdum sūmite poenās;

hōc Ithacus velit, et magnō mercentur Atrīdae.”

Urged to continue, he tells of the Greeks weariness of the
war, of their vain attempts to sail away, of the demand for
a victim to propitiate the angry gods, of his being selected
for this purpose, and of his esoape. Then he appeals for
their mercy.

Calchas advised a return and a new expedition under

The horse is taken into the city through a breach in the
walls. In spite of omens and the prophecies of Cassandra
Troy is given up to joyous celebration. At night, when
the Trojans, wearied and freed from anxiety, are asleep,
the Greeks return. Sinon opens the horse and the Greeks

take the city.

The ghost of Hector appears to Aeneas and warns him
to flee, taking with him the symbols of the Trojan religion,
and found a new city beyond the sea. Aeneas awakes,
hears the sounds of havoc and sees the flames destroying
Troy; he determines to fight. Panthus tells of the hopeless
resistance of the Trojans. ou

A number of Trojans join Aeneas. They are ready to
die with him. The horrors of that night are all about
them. First they meet the Greek Androgeos, who greets
them as friends. The Trojans overcome him.

friends attack them.

Outnumbered by the Greeks, the Trojans are slain or
scattered, only two remaining with Aeneas, one old, the
other wounded. At the palace of Priam they find the
fiercest fight. The Trojans hurl missiles from the roofs
at the Greeks, who use scaling ladders. One entrance is
left unguarded.

Aeneas, suddenly alarmed, flees ai full speed, forgetting

Aeneas calls Creusa and searches everywhere in vain.
At last her ghost appears, and tells him that she is to be
an attendant upon the goddess Cybele, not a slave of the
Greeks. Aeneas vainly grasps at her image. He returns
to his companions and starts for the mountains just as
day dawns.

QUESTIONS

Whnat is the general subject of this book?

As its events precede in time those of Book I, why did not Vergil
begin his poem with this book?

Is there any advantage in Vergil’s arrangement?

Did guests usually recline at banquets in Homer’s time? Did
the Romans in Vergil’s time?

How long did the Trojan war last?

Whno were the Myrmidons? How is the word used in English?
How is the English use related to the original meaning?

Under what ten headings could you summarize the chief events
described in this book?

Which of these would you select as the event most necessary to
the development of the story?

Whnere in this book does Vergil introduce the gods or omens into
the action of the poem?

To which of the gods did the Greeks look chiefly for assistance
in their plans for success at Troy?

Whno gives us this information in this book?

In what way did the Greeks lose the favor of this god?

What was the Palladium? How has this word been used in
English?

Whno devised the plan for building the wWooden Horse? Was
he also the builder?

How large a fleet did the Greeks send against Troy?

What significance may be attached to the fact that the serpents
which destroyed Laocoon came from Tenedos? And also
to the fact that after destroying him they fled to the statue
of Minerva?

Whnat caused Ulysses to hate Palamedes?

Just what was the cruel plot which Ulysses contrived in order
to avenge himself?

How does it serve Sinon’s purpose to introduce into his story
the reference to Palamedes and then to the seisure of the
Palladium?

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK II q7

How does the mention of these help him to deceive the Trojans?

Whnat words for Greek either as adjective or noun does Vergil
use in this book? Whnich does he employ most frequently?
Why are the Greeks called by this name?

Whnhat names does Vergil employ for Troy and Trojan?

Through which of their ancestors is a connection with Italy
establisheū for the Trojans?

If Achilles may be spoken of as representing the “strength” of
the Greek expedition, by what name may we fittingly de-
scribe Ulysses? Why? Whnat do the, epithets duri (v.7)
and pellacis (v. 90) imply about his character?

Whnho were the Atridae and what was their connection with the
expedition?

Why are serpents so generally associated with Minerva?

Of what arts was Minerva the patroness?

Can you see a reason why Vergil should choose to use the word
intexunt to describe the building of the horse?

By what other names is Minerva known?

Was any test made by the Trojans to see whether Greeks : were
concealed in the horse?

Had Thymoetes any private reason for urging the dragging of
the horse within the city?

What maiden is said to have been sacrificed by the Greeks to
secure a favorable voyage to Troy? What was her story
in full?

Which of Priam’s children had the gift of prophecy? For which
of them did this power prove futile?

Why is it especially suitable that Hector should advise Aeneas
not to sacrifice his life vainly but flee to a new land?
What bearing upon the connection between Troy and Rome is
suggested by the objects committed by Hector to the care
of Aeneas—the sacra, Penates, Vesta, and the ‘“ever-burning

fire”?

What successive causes drive Aeneas into the fighting despite
the warning of Hector’s ghost? How is he separated from
his companions?

Whnat turns his thoughts toward his home in the midst of the
fighting?

How do his feelings of resentment towsrd Helen indirectly lead
to his coming quickly and safely to his father’s house?

78 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK II

By what argument is he made to see the folly of further resis-
tance to the will of fate?

On what grounds does his father Anchises refuse to flee? Whnich
of his words are strangely at variance with ancient ideas?
How is his refusal to depart overcome?

BOOK nīI

Post eversa Phrygum regna ut fuga coepta moveri,
utque sit in Thracen primo devectus ibique

moenia condiderit Polydori caede piata,

regis et hospitium, ut Phoebi responsa canebant
coeptum iter in Cretam; rursus nova fata reperta,
naufragus ut fuerit Strophadas compulsus ad undas,
inde fugam et dirae narrat praecepta Celaenus,
liquerit utque Helenum praeceptis ordine fatis,
supplicem Achaemeniden Polyphemo urgente recepit,
amissumque patrem Drepani, atque hinc inde quievit.

THE STORY OF THE THIRD BOOK

Aeneas continues his story. The refugees build a fleet
and set sail at the beginning of summer. They go first
to Thrace, where they plan to found a new city. As
Aeneas tries to pull up bushes, in order to adorn the
altar on which he is sacrificing to Venus, blood flows from
them and a voice cries out that he is rending the body of
Polydorus. He was a son of Priam, who had been sent
with a large sum of money to be brought up by Lycurgus,
king of Thrace, and had been slain by the treacherous
king. Aeneas performs new funeral rites for Polydorus
and leaves the ill-omened spot.

Next they sail to Delos. As Aeneas prays to Apollo
in his temple, a voice replies that the Trojans must seek
their ancient mother. Since Anchises interprets this to
mean Crete, the motherland of Teucer,! the Trojans joy-
fully offer sacrifice to the gods and set sail. Arriving in
Crete, Aeneas begins a city, which he calls Pergamum;
but in the midst of their preparations drought and a pes-
tilence fall upon them. Then Anchises urged a return to
Delos to consult the oracle again; but in the night the
Phrygian Penates appeared to Aeneas and told him that
Apollo meant Hesperia, or Italy, the land from which
Dardanus was sprung. Aeness offers sacrifice, after which
he tells Anchises what he has seen and heard. The father
realizes his error and remembers that Cassandra often
spoke of Hesperia and Italy. The Trojans once more put
to sea.

1See Appendix, 20.

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK III 81

Vhen the wanderers are out of sight of land a storm
arises; they are driven from their course and wander
blindly for three days. Then they reach, the Strophades
islands, the abode of the Harpies. They land, slay cattle
and goats, and prepare a meal; but when they would eat,
the Harpies appear again and again, and spoil the food
by their unclean touch. When the Trojans lay an am-
buscade for the foul creatures, their chief, Celaeno, pre-
dicts such dire famine when they shall reach Italy that
they will be forced to devour their very tables. The Tro-
jans are panic-stricken, and Anchises prays to the gods
to avert the omen. They then sail along the coast of
Greece and reach a town near Mount Leucate and the
shrine of Apollo. They offer rites of purification to Jupi-
ter and celebrate games; Aeneas sets up a votive shield.
They then put to sea and come to Buthrotum, where they
find Helenus, Priam’s son, wedded to Andromache and
ruling a miniature Troy. Andromache is offering sacri-
fice at a tomb which she has dedicated to Hector. She
tells her story and says that a part of the kingdom of
Pyrrhus has fallen to Helenus. Helenus welcomes his
countrymen and entertains them hospitably.

On the point of departure, Aeneas asks for directions
for his journey, and the seer tells him as much as he is
allowed to reveal. Their destined home is on the remoter
side of Italy; a white sow with a litter of thirty white
suckling pigs will mark the site of their city. They need
not fear the eating of their tables; the Fates and Apollo
will aid them. They must pray to the gods with veiled
head. It is necessary to make a long detour and take a
course to the left around Sicily, to avoid the fearful mon-
ster, Scylla, and the devouring whirlpool, Charybdis.
They must honor Juno; constant efforts to propitiate her

8S2 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK III

will at last prevail. At Cumae Aeness is to visit ,the
Sibyl, who will tell him how to meet or avoid the dangers
that await him in Italy.

AĀfter an affecting farewell, the Trojans sail northward
to the port nearest to Italy. From there they make an
early morning start and soon sight the new land, which
they hail with shouts of joy. They make a brief landing
near Minerva’s Height, where herds of grazing horses are
an omen of war, although Anchises sees hope also of peace.
They sail on past Tarentum, hear the roar of Charybdis
and turn leftward; but in the dark they drift to the home
of the Cyclopes.

The Trojans spend a night made terrible by the vol-
cano. Next morning a Greek appears, in wretched plight,
and begs to be taken away with them. He was one of
the companions of Ulysses, left behind in their panic
fligant. He tells them of the Cyclopes, huge giants with
one eye in the middle of their foreheads, and of Ulysses’
vengeance for the death of his comrades. The Trojans
flee the spot, taking the stranger with them. Sailing
back, they skirt the southern shore of Sicily, passing many
famous places. At Drepanum Anchises dies, a calamity
unforetold. Next they came to Dido’s realm, and at that
point Aeneas ends his story.

Aeneas continues his iale. After the downfall of their
city the survivors build ships and in the early summer leave
the desolate site of Troy.

Aeneas, horror-stricken, recalls that Priam had sent
Polydorus, with a great sum of money, to be reared by
Lycurgus, when Troy’s fortunes seemed hopeless. He
performs due funeral rites to the shade of the luckless
youth.

Neptune, to Apollo, and to the ”ephyrs, the Trojans set
sail for the island.

The Trojans hear that Idomeneus has left his kingdom
and deserted his city in Crete. They sail through the
Cyclades full of joy and hope, and begin a new settlement

on the Cretan shore.

In the midst of their preparations a drought and pesti-
lence fall upon them. Anchises bids them return to Delos
and again consult Apollo.

QUESTIONS

Where did Aeneas first land after leaving Troy? Whnhy?

Whnat led him to leave the place? Whnat ceremonies marked his
arrival and departure?

What was the next objective point? Whny?

Whnat advice did Apollo give at Delos? How did Anchises in-
terpret it?

Whny is Apollo given such prominence in the Aeneid?

What caused the Trojans to abandon Crete?

Whnhat made it unnecessary for them to return to Delos?

What were the reasons for making their home in Italy? What
were the ancient names of that country?

Whnho was Cassandra? Why were her prophecies not believed? *

What was the previous history of the Harpies? How did the
Trojans offend them? What was the result?

Whnere did the Trojans make their next stop? Why did Vergil
represent them as landing there?

Whnat did the Trojans find at their next stopping-place? Who
was Andromache? Who was Helenus?

Whnhat did Helenus prophesy? Whnat ceremony preceded his
prophecy?

Why did Andromache give special gifts to Ascanius?

How was Aeneas’ promise to Helenus of friendship and alliance
between their cities fulfilled?

Whnere was the next landing? For what purpose?

Whnat was the mythological explanation of the activity of Aetna?

Whnat adventure befel the Trojans at Aetna?

How had Ulysses avenged the death of his comrades?

Describe the course of the Trojans from Aetna to Drepanum
with the cities which they passed.

Whny is the shore at Drepanum called joyless?

How does Vergil make the story of Aeneas’ wanderings inter-
esting and poetic? How is his mission emphasized?

BOOK IV

At regina gravi Veneris iam carpitur igni.
Consulitur soror Anna; placet succumbere amori;
fiunt sacra deis, onerantur numina donis.

Itur venatum; Veneris clam foedera iungunt;

facti fama volat; monitus tunc numine divum
Aeneas classemque fugae sociosque parabat.

Sensit amans Dido precibus conata morari.
Postquam fata iubent, nec iam datur ulla facultas,
conscenditque pyram dixitque novissima verba

et vitam infelix multo cum sanguine fudit.

THE STORY OF THE FOURTH BOOK

Within this book we find a complete drama, of which
the scenes may easily be marked off. Anna, Dido’s sis-
ter, learning that Dido is inclined to yield to a growing
passion for Aeneas, encourages her to do so, declaring that
her fidelity to the memory of Sychaeus, her former hus-
band, has been amply shown, and that the dead are not
at all concerned with such fidelity. Furthermore, she de-
scribes the advantages for Carthage of such a marriage,
and thus adds to the unrest in Dido’s heart. The queen,
almost maddened by her feelings, devotes herself entirely
to entertaining Aeneas, abandoning the work upon her
new city. Meanwhile Juno reproaches Venus for having,
with the help of her son Cupid, caused Dido’s present
unhappiness; she proposes a marriage of Aeneas and Dido,

hoping thereby to keep Aeneas in Africa and so prevent

the ultimate founding of Rome. To this Venus readily
agrees, though not at all deceived in regard to Juno’s de-
signs. Then at a hunting party, whose members are

obliged by a storm to seek various places of shelter, the

marriage takes place. The goddess Rumor reports the
event to Iarbas, a rejected suitor for Dido’s hand, and he,
being the son of Jupiter Ammon, appeals to his father to
avenge him. Jupiter then sends Mercury to Aeneass, to
bid him leave Carthage and fulfil his destiny elsewhere.
Aeneas, startled by the apparition, prepares to obey, but
meanwhile Dido has become alarmed and hears through
the same goddess Rumor of the preparations for depar-
ture. She reproaches Aeneas bitterly, but always with
an implied entreaty that he remain with her. Aeneas in
113

114 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK IV

reply denies that she has any claim upon him which jus-
tifies his staying in Carthage. It is heaven’s decree, not
his own wish, that takes him away. Dido becomes fren-
zied at this; she tells Aeneas to go but assures him that
he will meet his due punisament. He persists, neverthe-
less, in his preparations for departure, and again Dido
sends Anna to him with appeals merely for a short post-
ponement of his voyage; but all without avail. At this
Dido gives up all hope; she cunningly plans a method of
self-destruction which should deceive her sister. She tells
her of certain mysterious sacred rites of which she has
heard, by the performance of which either she will be
freed from her love for Aeneas or he will be brought back
to her. Preparations for a pretended sacrifice are made,
and all the mementoes of Aeneas are collected. In a solil-
oquy, as if still undecided Dido considers whether to take
her Tyrians with her and, abandoning Carthage, join the
Trojans, or to kill herself; she decides on the latter alter-
native. Aeneas, warned again by Mercury, makes a hur-
ried departure during the night. At daybreak Dido dis-
covers that the Trojans have gone. At first she thinks of
pursuing and destroying them, but ends by invoking a
solemn curse upon Aeneas. She prays that the hatred
she now feels may be inherited by her people’s descendants
and that no peace may ever exist between the two nations:

“Fight shore with shore, fight sea with sea,
Fight all that are or eer shall be.”

She sends her sister away to fetch victims for the sacri-
fice, and, in her absence, mounts the funeral pile which
was to play a part in the pretended sacred rites, and stabs
herself. Iris, sent down by Juno, frees her struggling
spirit.

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK IV 115

Dido, deeply smitten with love for Aeneas, cannot rest.
She makes a confidant of her sister Anna, but also vows
eternal fidelity to the memory of her former husband,
Sychaeus.

At rēgīna gravī iamdūdum saucia cūrā

vulnus alit vēnīs, et caecō carpitur īgnī.

Multa virī virtūs animō, multusque recursat

gentis honōs; haerent īnfīxī pectore vultūs

verbaque, nec placidam membrīs dat cūra quiētem.
Postera Phoebēā lūstrābat lampade terrās,

ūmentemque Aurōra polō dīmōverat umbram,

cum sīc ūnanimam alloquitur male sāna sorōrem:

“ Anna soror, quae mē suspēnsam īnsomnia terrent!

Quis novus hīc nostrīs successit sēdibus hospes,

quem sēsē ōre ferēns, quam fortī pectore et armīs!

Crēdō equidem, nec vāna fidēs, genus esse deōrum:

dēgenerēs animōs timor arguit. Heu, quibus ille

iactātus fātīs! quae bella exhausta canēbat!

Sī mihi nōn animō fīxum immōtumque sedēret,

nē cui mē vinclō vellem sociāre iugālī,

postquam prīmus amor dēceptam morte fefellit;

sī nōn pertaesum thalamī taedaeque fuisset,

huic ūnī fōrsan potuī succumbere culpae.

Anna, fatēbor enim, miserī post fāta Sychaeī

coniugis et sparsōs fraternā caede Penātēs,

sōlus hic īnflēxit sēnsūs, animumque labantem

impulit: āgnōscō veteris vestīgia flammae.

Sed mihi vel tellūs optem prius īma dehīscat,

vel Pater omnipotēns adigat mē fulmine ad umbrās,

pallentēs umbrās Erebī noctemque profundam,

ante, Pudor, quam tē violō, aut tua iūra resolvō.

Anna implies that devotion to his memory does not con-
cern Sychaeus. She pictures the dangerous position of
the city. Safety and glory are assured by marriage with
Aeneas. She believes the gods favor this, and suggests
excuses for delaying Aeneas.

Meantime Juno, seeing Dido’s condition, proposes to
Venus that they agree upon her marriage to Aeneas. The
proposal is accepted, and they plan to interrupt by a severe
storm the hunting party to be held the following day.

Rumor is described, an evil being sent from the Lower
World to plague mankind. She carries the neuws of the
marriage everywhere. Thus Iarbas, whom Dido has re-
jected as a suitor, hears of it.

Iarbas, son of Jupiter Hammon, appeals to the king of
the gods, declaring that he is unjust, or blind, not to punish
Aeneus and Dido. He likens Aeneas to Paris carrying
off Helen. Jupiter sends Mercury to warn Aeneas to
leave Carthage.

Mercury, wearing his winged sandals and carrying his
wand, descends to Carthage, first alighting on Mount Atlas.
He gives his message and departs.

Aeneas, startled by this divine command, prepares to

At last she upbraids him, yet begs him to abandon his
departure. She describes her unhappy position, with
fresh enemies made on his account. Aeneas in his de-
fense declares that the gods will not permit him to do as he
would like.

Aeneas may seek a new home, even as Dido has done.
Not to do so is to rob Ascanius. Dido again reproaches
him for his hardness of heart. She tells what she has
done for him and ridicules his plea that the gods have
an especial interest in him. She prays that he may
perish and says that her avenging shade uwill follow him.

At night Dido, sleepless, debates with herself what to do.
Shall she turn to other suitors whom she has scorned?
Shall she beg the Trojans to take her alone with them?
Shall she join the Trojans with all her Tyrians? Death

is preferable. She is utterly desperate.

The attendants hurry to her. Lamentation fills the
city. Anna returning sees how she has been misled. She
vainly attempts to staunch the flowing blood. Iris, sent
down by Juno, releases Dido’s struggling spirit, and the

tragedy ends.

QUESTIONS

How might the Fourth Book be divided into scenes, to show its
dramatic quality?

Who were Pygmalion? Sychaeus? Iarbas? What part has
each in the story of Dido? Whnich of the three claimed a
divine parent? How does Vergil use this fact in developing
his plot?

What arguments does Anna employ to induce Dido to wed
Aeneas?

What fitness can you see in describing Ceres as legiferae?

What chief point of resemblance is to be emphasized in the
sīmile of the wounded deer, as applied to the relations of
Aeneas and Dido? (Cf. vv. 69-73.)

How does Dido expect to influence Aeneas by emphasizing the
fact that her city is paratam (v. 75)?

Why does Venus acquiesce so readily in Juno’s proposal that
Aeneas and Dido should marry? Why did not Venus fear
that the outcome would fulfil Juno’s hopes?

In what three places of the story does the personified Rumor
play a part?

Who is meant by Cyllenius and Cyllenia proles? WwWhy? Bry
what phrase was the god described in the First Book?

At what two points in the action does Mercury appear? By
what two attributes may he be distinguished from the other
gods?

Do the duties of Iris in any way resemble those of Mercury?
What duties did he have other than those of a messenger?

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK IV 143

How did the king Atlas become the mountain Atlas, which Ver-
gil describes?

Whnhat was the feeling of the followers of Aeneas in regard to
leaving Carthage at this time?

What meaning has Baccho in v. 302? Is Bacchus represented
as calling?

Whnat is the usual meaning of the word Bacchus when not used
as the name of the god?

Whnere is Cithaeron and why is it chosen as the place of these
revels?

Whnat point is there in Dido’s reference to the storminess of the
sea? Is she concerned for Aeneas’ safety? Is his answer
satisfactory from a modern view-point? Just what is the
ancient conception of duty under the circumstances?

Whnat places were the most celebrated sites of Oracles of Apollo?

What other powers besides that of prophecy were especially
attributed to Apollo?

Whnat circumstances of Vergil’s boyhood explain his frequent
selection of ants, bees, and snakes as the subjects of his
similes?

Can you establish any connection in origin between the two
words hostis (v. 424) and hospes (v. 323) which Dido applies
to Aeneas? Cf. the meaning of hospita (3, 539).

Where was Aulis and what happened there?

Who was charged with having molested the ashes of Anchises?

How may we understand the use of pulchro (v. 432) with Latio,
coming from the lips of Dido at this time?

In what way is Dido to repay Aeneas by dying? Or (if we refer
the words to her sister) to repay Anna?

What Latin names for the Wwinds can you mention? What
other name has the same meaning as Boreas?

Whnose tears are meant in v. 49? To what different persons
might they be attributed? Does the position of mens and
of lacrimae in the verse have any bearing on the meaning?

With whose story are the Eumenides chiefly associated?

For what fault did Pentheus suffer?

Whnat poets have written plays upon the tragic story of Orestes?

What is implied by the introduction of the word scaenis (v. 471)
and in limine (v. 473)?

144 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK IV

What Athenian Court (mentioned in the Bible) was said to
have been established for the trial of Orestes?

What was the exact meaning and origin of the term FEumenides?
By what other names were the Dirae (v. 473) known?

Whnat was the Homeric conception of the Oceanus (v. 480)?

What is the origin of the word temple? Does not the original
meaning indicate that Vergil, by the word temple, might
mean just what others do by garden of the Hesperides?
Whnat Greek hero secured possession of the Golden Apples?
Give the whnole story.

Does not v. 486 seem somewhat out of place, in view of the
dragon’s duties?

How does the meaning of mugire (v. 490) compel a change from
the usual meaning of videbis?

What can you say of the probable origin of such a word as
mugire? Name the figure employed.

With what two divinities other than Diana is Hecate sometimes
identified?

Whnat and where was Avernus (cf. 3, 442)?

What relation to the effectiveness of the appeal to the gods had
the introduction into the ceremonial of objects most diffi-.
cult to secure (vv. 512-5315)?

Whnat one word in 44 ff. implies that Dido was thinking at the
moment of an attack on the Trojan fleet?

Does the phrase quisquis es (v. ē7ī) necessarily imply that Aeneas

did not know what god had come to him?

What was the story of Tithonus?

Do you know of any character in mythology who is said to have
practised the form of vengeance mentioned in v. 602?

Why was Hecate sometimes called Trivia?

Whnhat were the Sortes Vergilianae? What famous instance of
their use in a period not very distant from to-day?

Was there any act in a regular sacrifice which corresponded to
the removal of a lock of Dido’s hair by Iris?

THE STORY OF THE FIFTH BOOK

The Trojans, sailing toward Italy, are still near enough
to Africa to see the smoke and flames arising from Dido’s
funeral pyre, and they feel some misgivings. A storm
gathers and the winds are adverse, so they decide to land
in Sicily, where they are warmly welcomed by their former
host Acestes. A year has elapsed since the death of
Anchises. His tomb is in Sicily. In commemoration of
this anniversary Aeneas determines to hold games and
invites the Sicilians to participate in them. Proper cere-
monials are performed at the tomb, and the ninth day
later is appointed for the games. The prizes are exhib-
ited. The first contest is a race between four of the
swiftest Trojan ships, all with characteristic figureheads,
as their names show. The race is out to sea and back,
after rounding a mark some distance off shore. The ex-
citement is great and the race resembles that of chariots
in the circus. Recklessly one captain in anger hurls an
overcautious pilot into the sea, and thus loses his chance
of winning. Another steers his ship too close to a rock
in making the turn and runs upon the reef. At last Clo-
anthus, in the Scylla, wins; Mnestheus, with the Pristis,
takes second place, having passed the two who had met
with mishaps, and almost comes in first. Most conspicu-
ous among the contestants in the foot-race which follows
are the two Trojan boys, Nisus and Euryalus, famed for
their affection for each other. Nisus, who was leading
in the race, when near the finish loses his footing on the
slippery ground and falls. But by rising and upsetting

146

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK V 147

an Acarnanian who is following him closely in second
place, he enables his friend Euryalus to win. After this
comes the boxing match, for which a young Trojan called
Dares immediately enters. For a time no opponent ap-
pears. Seeing this, Acestes induces Entellus, an old
friend of his, to take up the challenge. Dares, who is
much the younger and the lighter of the two, at the first
has all the best of the combat. Entellus, in striving to
reach Dares with a heavy blow, loses his footing and falls
to the ground. Txnis simply rouses him, however, and he
pummels Dares cruelly, until Aeneas is compelled to with-
draw the Trojan from the fight, comforting him by declar-
ing that some god is aiding Entellus. Then Entellus dis-
plays his strength by killing with a single blow of his fist
the bullock which stood by as the prize for the winner of
the contest. After this exhibition Entellus gives up box-
ing forever. Then comes a contest in archery. The dove
which, tied to a mast, serves as a mark for the competi-
tors, is almost struck by one contestant who hits the mast;
the next severs the cord and releases the bird; a third kills
the bird in mid-air. After this Acestes sends his arrow
into the clouds, whereupon it takes fire and is entirely
consumed. Acestes is adjudged the winner of the match
and is fittingly honored. After this there is a surprise
for the spectators. It consists of a series of intricate
manceuvres by the Trojan boys on horseback under the
leadership of Iulus. So involved are the figures they de-
scribe and so difficult to follow are their movements, that
the scene is compared to the play of a school of dolphins in
the sea or to the windings and maszy passages of the Cretan
Labyrinth. Whnile this is going on, Juno, bent as ever
upon making trouble for the Trojans, sends her messen-
ger Iris down to the women, who, apart from the men,

148 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK V

were mourning for Anchises. Assuming the form of one
of their number who was not with them at the time, Iris
incites them to burn the ships, so that they may not be
forced to wander further over the sea. Word of this
reaches the field where the boys are riding, and the Tro-
jans hurry away to the blazing vessels. They fight the
fire in vain, until Aeneas makes a despairing prayer to
Jupiter; the god sends a heavy rain, and all but four of
the ships are saved from the flames. Soon after this
Aeneas is advised by one of his oldest councillors to leave
behind him in Sicily all the weakest part of his followers,
founding for them the city of Acesta, named in honor of
Acestes. Later, in a dream, the ghost of Anchises appears
to Aeneas and gives the same advice, telling him, further-
more, that before he founds his Italian city he is to make
a visit to his father in the Lower World, guided there by
a Sibyl. Then there is further feasting, after which, with
the advent of favorable weather, they sail for Italy. This
time Neptune, to whom Venus makes a special appeal for
a safe voyage, brings them to the promised land. On the
way, however, they lose Palinurus, the pilot of Aeneas’
ship, who is overcome by the god of sleep and thrown
overboard with a part of the tiller to which he clung.
Aeneas is roused by the unsteady movement of the ship
and himself acts as pilot.

As he voyages toward Italy Aeneas encounters head
winds. Palinurus, the pilot, suggests landing in Sicily,
as he cannot make headuay against the gale.

The Trojans offer sacrifices at the tomb of Anchises. A
serpent appears from beneath the tomb and feeds upon the
offerings. This is regarded as a sign of Anchises’ ap-
proval, and the ceremonies are continued and a feast held
in his honor.

The ninth day is propitious. The Sicilians attend and
some enter the lists of contestants in the games. The priaes
are exhibited. The first “event,” the boat race, has four
entries, Pristis, Chimaera, Centaur, and Scylla. The
race is to be out to sea, rounding a rock, and return.

Sergestus attempt to pass him also. Mnestheus gains, but

the reef and breaks many of his oars. Mnestheus passes

Gyas.

Then Mnestheus, amid great shouting and excitement,

Cloanthus is proclaimed winner, and in addition to the
laurel wreath receives a special priae, a cloak embroidered
with the story of Ganymede. Mnestheus receives a coat
of mail. All the crews have gifts of food and money.
When Centaur comes in crippled, Aeneas also rewards
her conmander for having saved his ship and men.

Tum satus Anchīsā, cūnctīs ex mōre vocātīs,

victōrem magnā praecōnis vōce Cloanthum 245
dēclārat, viridīque advēlat tempora laurō;

vīnaque et argentī magnum dat ferre talentum.

The foot-race followvs. Among the contestants are the
devoted friends, Nisus and Euryalus. Aeneas announces

Euryalus, Helymus, and Diores, the last two nearly
abreast.

Nisus falls, but recovers sufficiently to upset Salius in-
tentionally, so that Euryalus may win. Helymus takes
second, Diores third place. Salius protests without avail.
Houwever, Aeneas gives him a special priage, whereupon the
innocent Nīisus asserts that he and Salius were alike un-
lucky. Nisus also gets a special priae.

Then comes the boxing match. Dares, the Trojan,
enters, young, strong, and confident. No one dares face
him, whereupon he demands the priae. At this Acestes
reproaches his old friend, Entellus, who in spite of his
years decides to fight, and flings into the ring the huge
cestus which he had used years before with Eryx, his
famous teacher. j

Entellus, yielding to protests, agrees to use other cestus,
neither his own nor Trojan. The battle opens. Entellus
is old and heavy, Dares young and active. The former,
aiming a heavy blow, misses his aim and falls, but is merely
aroused by his mishap.

Ante omnēs stupet ipse Darēs longēque recūsat;
magnanimusque Anchīsiadēs et pondus et ipsa

hūc illūc vinclōrum immēnsa volūmina versat..

He beats and pummels Dares till Aeneas stops the fight,
soothing Dares by saying that some god has intervened
to help Entellus, so that it is folly to contend longer. The
priaes are awvarded. Entellus slays the bullock, his prixe,
with a single blow, and announces that this is his last fight.

Tum pudor incendit vīrēs et cōnscia virtūs, 455
praecipitemque Darēn ārdēns agit aequore tōtō,
nunc dextrā ingemināns ictūs, nunc ille sinistrā;
nec mora, nec requiēs; quam multā grandine nimbī

The archers have for a mark a dove fastened to a mast.

Hyrtacides, Mnestheus, Eurytion, and Acestes enter.

The

first strikes the mast. Mnestheus cuts the cord. Eurytion
kills the dove as it flies. Acestes shoots an arrow never-
theless, which takes fire in the heavens. This is taken as
an omen of Jupiter’s favor, and Acestes gets the first priae.

Ascanius and his boy friends, under the guidance of
Eputides, perform intricate manoeuvres on horseback be-
fore their parents and the general gathering. The exhibi-
tion is planned as a surprise for the spectators. Several
Roman families are complimented by Vergil, who intro-
duces as leaders in this equestrian game boys who are said

to be the ancestors of those families.

Various movements and counter movements and a sham
battle are presented. These manoeuvres are as intricate
and involved as the passages of the famous labyrinth of
Crete. The boys sport about as alert as dolphins, and
cross and recross each other’s paths. This game, con-
tinued by their descendants, is known in Verqils day.

Meantime Iris, sent by Juno, finds the Trojan women
bemoaning the lot which drives them over the sea in search
of a city. She assumes the form of Beroe, and, rousing
them to madness, urges them to burn the ships and end
their wanderings. She flings a torch at the ships. One
of the women detects her disguise.

The women hesitate, but Iris has vanished into the heav-
ens and they wait no longer. The ships are fired. A mes-
senger telling of the disaster reaches the scene of the game.
Ascanius appeals to the women, who are frightened and
flee. The efforts of the men being unavailing, Aeneas ap-
peals to heaven; a heavy rain-storm follows, which puts

out the flames and saves all the ships except four.

Aeneas is advised to found a city and leave behind the
feeblest and least brave of his followers. The ghost of An-
chises, appearing in his sleep, urges him to accepti this ad-
vice, and tells him that he must descend to the Lower World
and visit his father; the Sibyl will conduct him, and he
will be informed about his descendants and his city.

The ships are refitted, a new colony founded, a temple

Venus intercedes with Neptune in behalf of her son, and
begs that he may be allowed this time to reach Italy in
safety. Neptune agrees, but says that one of the number

will perish. He rides over the waters and quiets them.

At Venus intereā Neptūnum exercita cūrīs
alloquitur, tālēsque effundit pectore questūs:

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK V 179

cōgunt mē, Neptūne, precēs dēscendere in omnēs;
quam nec longa diēs, pietās nec mītigat ūlla,

nec Iovis imperiō Fātīsque īnfrācta quiēscit.

Nōn mediā dē gente Phrygum exēdisse nefandīs 185

rēliquiās Troiae, cinerēs atque ossa perēmptae
īnsequitur. Causās tantī sciat illa furōris.

The fleet is guided on its way by Palinurus, the pilot of
Aeneas’ vessel. Somnus, the god of sleep, overcomes
Palinurus, and he falls overboard. Aeneas awakens, takes
the place of the pilot, and steers the ship safely en.

‘1382 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK V

QUESTIONS

In what part of Sicily was the city of Egesta or Segesta? How
is it to be associated with the fortunes of the Trojans?
What origin of the name does Vergil give?

What superstitious feeling led to the change of the name Egesta
to Segesta? Whnat connection had the place in historical
times with the Peloponnesian War between Athens and
Sparta?

Were funeral games celebrated in historical times? From what
writer does Vergil get his model for these games? In whose
honor and by whom were those earlier games celebrated?

Is the list of games, as forecast by Aeneass, carried out in full?

Is the phrase Phaethontis equi (v. 105) correctly employed?

Why are tripods called sacri?

Whnat other metal, besides those mentioned in v. 112, has been
most dommonly used as a basis for money? What metal
was first used at Rome for this purpose, as shown by the
Latin word for treasury?

Whnat is the fundamental meaning of talentum?

Whnat form of ship must these racers have had (cf. v. 120)? Is
Vergil justified in introducing such ships here, or is it an
anachronism? By whom were such ships first used?

How would you define the word insigne, as applied to a ship?

Whnat characteristics have the insignia of the four racing vessels
in common?

Why were poplar leaves chosen in making wreaths for these
athletes?

What help in picturing the scene of the race is given by the de-
scriptive phrases inclusa litora and pulsati colles?

How did the shape and operation of the rudder of an ancient
ship differ from that of our own ships?

Whnat distinction would you make between clavus (v. 177) and
gubernaclo (v. 176)?

Whnhat was the exact location of the Gaetulian Syrtes, the Ionian
Sea, and Malea? Does Mnestheus refer to them in the
order in which the Trojans in their voyage had encountered
them? Account for the order he follows.

Is it likely that the Trojans really reached either of the Syrtes?

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK V 183

What may we suppose was the favorite point of sailing for the
men of the heroic period?

Whnat is there singularly appropriate about the descriptive term
sequacibus in v. 193? How does the derivation and the
usual force of the suffix bear this out?

What propriety is there in using the expression aerea puppis (v.
198), unless the ancients built ships of copper as we do ours

of steel?

If voti reus (v. 237) is identical in meaning with voti damnatus,
how can a person so described be a successful, happy person?

Whnat is the origin of our English word meander (cf. v. 251)?

What was the story of the puer regius (cf. v. 252)?

How is Aeneas indirectly praised by this description of the
shield of Demoleos and of Demoleos himself (cf. vv. 259-
265)?

Whnat figure of speech is illustrated by fama obscura (cf. v. 302)?

How would the trick by which Nisus enabled his friend Euryalus
to win the race be regarded in modern racing?

Does this reference to Paris agree with most estimates of him
(cf. v. 370)?

Whnat was the form of the ancient caestus? Can they properly
be described as gloves?

What difference is there in purpose between the wearing of mod-
ern boxing gloves and the wearing of the ancient caestus?

Whnat difference in the effect of blows results from increasing
the size and weight of modern boxing gloves? Is it the
same with the caestus?

In what sense is aemula true of old age? Of what is it jealous?

What was the method of drawing lots with the use of a helmet?
What light is thrown on this question by the words deiectam
(v. 490), accepit (v. 491), exit (v. 492), and subsedit (v. 498)?

In how many of the four events of this athletic programme do
the gods affect the result? Why does it seem especially
fitting that all reference to them should be omitted in the
second event?

What historical reason has Vergil for introducing the Game of

Troy at this festival? Why does he introduce Atys as one
of the leaders?

Whno bulilt the Cretan Labyrinth and for what purpose?

184 VERGCIL’S AENEID.— BOOK V :

In what way can Neptune be said to suggest to the Trojan
women the burning of the ships?

Whnat parallel to this visit of Aeneas to the Lower World does
Homer offer?

How many Sibyls were there? Whnich was the most celebrated?
Which Sibyl acted as the guide for Aeneas?

How was Neptune connected with the building of the walls of
Troy?

Describe a ship of the Homeric era.

Whnat does Aeneas find particularly bitter in the fate of Pali-
nurus? Whnat inconsistency do you find here with the
utterance of Anchises in 2, 646?

Which feeling is more appropriate to ancient beliefs? Why?

BOOK VI

Cumas deinde venit. Fert hinc responsa Sibyllae;
Misenum sepelit; mons servat nomen humati.
Ramum etiam ante deum placato numine portat,
at vates longaeva una descendit Avernum.
Agnoscit Palinurum et ibi solatur Elissam,
Deiphobumque videt lacerum crudeliter ora;
umbrarum poenas discit narrante Sibylla.
Convenit Anchisen penitusque in valle virenti
agnoscitque suam prolem monstrante parente.
Haec ubi percepit, graditur sociosque revisit.

THE STORY OF THE SIXTH BOOK

The Trojans arrive at Cumae; Aeneas seeks Apollo’s
height and the abode of the inspired Sibyl. On the doors
of Phoebus’ temple is pictured the whole story of the
Minotaur, excepting the fall of Icarus, which the father’s
hand could not depict. As Aeneas is gazing at these
scenes he is summoned to the temple, at the back of
which is a huge cavern, the Sibyl’s abode. As she enters
the shrine, the priestess is transformed and inspired by
the god. She calls upon Aeneas to pray; he begs Apollo
to pity them and allow them to settle in Latium, where
he promises honors for Phoebus, Trivia, and the Sibyl.
Finally mastered by the god, the Sibyl prophesies that
the Trojans shall safely reach Latium, but there wars
and carnage await them, again due to a foreign bride.
Aeneas must, however, be bold and not bow to Fortune.
He replies that he is ready to face any dangers and hard-
ships; he begs the Sibyl to allow him to visit his father
Anchises and to show him the way to the Lower World.
The priestess replies that the descent to Avernus is easy;
it is the return that is difficult. If he is resolved to make
the attempt, he must first find and pluck the golden
bough, as a gift to Proserpina. Furthermore, one of his
comrades is dead, though Aeneas knows it not; he must
first be buried, then an offering of black cattle must be
made to the infernal powers.

As Aeneas returns sadly to the ships, he finds his dead
comrade in the person of the trumpeter, Misenus. He
had been making the sea ring with a hollow shell and

186

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK VI 187

challenging the gods to a contest, when Triton, in jealousy,
drowned him. The Trojans cut down trees for a funeral
pyre. Two doves guide Aeneas to the sought-for tree; he
easily plucks the bough and bears it back to the Sibyl.
The last rites are paid to Misenus; Aeneas erects his tomb
at the foot of a high mount, which still bears the trum-
peter’s name.

Aeneas and the Sibyl, after sacrificing black victims to
the gods below, enter Avernus. After asking permission
to reveal the secrets of the Lower World, Vergil describes
their journey. Passing by Grief, Care, Discord, and other
dread forms, and the shades of centaurs and other mon-
sters, they reach the river which the ferryman Charon
guards. About him throng men, women, and children,
many in number as the leaves or the migrating birds,
and beg to be allowed to cross. Only those who have
had due funeral rites are ferried across; the others must
wander on the bank for a hundred years. Among the
latter Aeneas sees the captain of his lost ship, and Pali-
nurus, who tells his story. As Aeneas and his companion
draw near, Charon warns them away; but the Sibyl tells
him that they have no evil purpose and shows him the
golden bough. He then lands them on the other side.
There they lull Cerberus to sleep with a drugged cake,
traverse the abodes of the untimely dead, the unjustly
condemned and suicides, and reach the dwelling-place of
those who died for love. Here Aeneas finds Dido and
tries to propitiate her, but she leaves him without a word.
In the abode of famous warriors he is greeted by many of
his friends, while the Greeks flee in terror, vainly trying
. to shout. He sees Deiphobus, cruelly mutilated, and
hears his story.

They reach the spot where the ways to Elysium and

188 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK VI

Tartarus divide. The Sibyl describes the horrors of Tar-
tarus, where many notorious criminals are suffering pun-
isnment. They reach the palace of Proserpina and leave
the golden bough. In the Elysian Fields they find the
good, amid delightful surroundings, enjoying the sports
and pleasures which they loved on earth. Anchises is
surveying the spirits that are to return to the upper air
and are drinking of Lethe’s stream. From a high mound
he points out to his son those who are to be the descen-
dants of the Trojans: the rulers of Alba, the kings and
heroes of early Rome, Pompey, Julius Caesar, and Augus-
tus. The Romans, he says, will conquer Argos and
Myecenae and avenge the destruction of Troy. Other
peoples will surpass them in the arts and sciences; Rome’s
mission will be to secure peace by conquest. He points
out Marcellus, who is to conquer Hannibal and the Gauls.
With him is the young Marcellus, the adopted son of
Augustus, doomed to an early death, to whom Vergil pays
an eloquent tribute. After rousing his son’s ardor by re-
vealing the great future of the race which he is to found,
Anchises dismisses him by the ivory gate.

The Trojans arrive at Cumae, land, and explore the
new country. Aeneas seeks Apollo’s height and the abode
of the prophetic Sibyl, who is inspired by the Delian god.

Apollo’s temple was built by Daedalus after his escape

taur, all but the fall of Icarus. The priestess bids Aeneas

Sibyl.

Wohen the priestess has finished, Aeneas replies that no
form of toil is unlooked for; he begs that she will show him
the way to the Lower World and to his father, Anchises,
whom he saved from the flames of Troy.

Anchises had bidden him ask this favor, which the Sibyl

Quīn, ut tē supplex peterem et tua līmina adīrem, uis

The priestess replies that it is easy to descend to Hades;
it is the return that is difficult. She describes the dangers
of the journey; one must first find and pluck the golden
bough as a gift to Proserpina.

Tālibus ōrābat dictīs, ārāsque tenēbat,

First they encounter the three-headed dog Cerberus and

They traverse the abodes of the untimely dead; of the

Continuō audītae vōcēs vāgītus et ingēns

prōiēcēre animās. Quam vellent aethere in altō

alligat, et noviēns Styx interfūsa coercet.

Among these last Aeneas finds Dido and tries to pro-
pitiate her, swearing that he left Carthage unuillingly.
She remains obdurate, and at last leaves him without a
word and goes to Sychaeus, her former husband.

prōsequitur lacrimīs longē, et miserātur euntem.

In the abode of famous warriors Aeneas finds many
friends and foes. The Trojan shades throng about him
and question him; the Greeks flee before him, as they did
on the plains of Troy, vainly trying to shout.

Inde datum mōlītur iter. Iamque arva tenēbant
ultima, quae bellō clārī sēcrēta frequentant.
Hīc illī occurrit Tȳdeus, hīc inclutus armīs

Here Aeneas sees Deiphobus, cruelly mutilated, and
asks what has befallen him, saying that he had heard
of his death and had performed such rites as he could.
Deiphobus replies that his condition is the work of Helen.

As they talk, the Sibyl reminds Aeneas of the flight of
time. They have reached the place where the roads to
Elysium and Tartarus divide. Deiphobus leaves them,
wishing Aeneas better fortune than his own.

question the Sibyl describes the horrors of Tartarus.

They come to the Elysian Fields; there, amid beautiful
surroundings, the good enjoy the pleasures that they loved

of Troy, with others feasting and singing paeans.

Deep in a green vale Anchises was surveying the spirits

scendants and considering their fortunes and deeds. He
welcomes his son, who vainly tries to embrace him.

Aeneas sees countless spirits, who are soon to return to
earth, flocking like bees about Lethe’s streem. Anchises
explains that they are drinking forgetfulness of the nast.
Aeneas wonders at their longing for earth.

Anchises explains that man is made up of body and
spirit, and tells how the souls are purified after death. The
good then spend a thousand years in Elysium, after which
they return to earth again.

“Dīcam equidem, nec tē suspēnsum, nāte, tenēbō”;
suscipit Anchīsēs, atque ōrdine singula pandit.

noble forms, crowned with the civic oak.

adversōs legere, et venientum discere vultus. 755

He enumerates the cities which they are to found; then
