224 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK VI

QUESTIONS

Whnere did Aeneas make his first landing in Italy?

What made the place famous?

How did Daedalus reach Cumae from Crete?

Who was the Cumaean Sibyl? How did she usually make her
prophecies? How did she make them to Aeneas? Whnat
were the Sibylline Books? What was their importance in
Roman religion?

What vow did Aeneas make to induce the Sibyl to prophesy?
Was the vow fulfilled? What characteristic feature of
Roman religion is shown by the custom of making vows?

Whnat did Apollo predict?

What offering was Aeneas expected to take to Proserpina?
Whno was she and what was her story?

For what two reasons was the burial of Misenus important?

How had he met his death? What other examples in the Aeneid
indicate the gods’ possession of human weaknesses?

What kind of victims were offered to the powers of the Nether
World?

Why does Vergil, in speaking of the deeds of Aeneas, emphasize
the help of the gods rather than his hero’s courage and
wisdom?

Describe the funeral of Misenus. On what does the ceremony
throw light?

Whnere did Aeneas enter the Lower World? Were there other
entrances? What explanation is given of the name Aver-
nus?

What must be done before entering the Lower World?

Whnat does Vergil mean by the “vestibule” of Pluto’s realm?
Whnat did he find there?

Whno was Charon and what were his duties? How does Vergil
describe him?

On what conditions were spirits taken across the Stygian Pool?

Whom did Aeneas recognize among those who were not per-
mitted to cross?

How did the Sibyl console Palinurus?

Why did Charon at first refuse to take Aeneas into his boat?
How was he persuaded to do so?

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOK VI 225

Describe Cerberus. How was he quieted?

Describe Aeneas’ progress through the Shades?

Whnere did he find Dido? How did he greet her and how was
his explanation received?

Whom did Aeneas meet in the realm of departed heroes?

What was the fate of Deiphobus?

Why did not the Sibyl show Aeneas the sights of Tartarus?
How did the Sibyl know about them? How much of Tar-
tarus did Aeneas see?

What crimes and criminals does the Sibyl describe? Whnat kind
of crimes are given special emphasis?

Describe the Elysian Fields. Whom does Aeneas find there?
What virtues are emphasized?

What was Anchises doing? What was the treatment of spirits
destined for Elysium? What must they do before return-
ing to the world above?

Whnat heroes does Anchises point out? Why does he do this?

Did the Romans of Vergil’s day believe in a Lower World such
as Vergil describes? Will a simple “yes” or ‘“no” answer
this question? Why?

OUTLINE OF BOOKS VII TO XII

After his return from the Nether World Aeneas sails
northward along the coast. Ōn the way his nurse dies
and her name is given to the promontory of Caieta (mod-
ern Gaeta). The Trojans pass the domain of Circe by
night, hearing the roars of the wild beasts into which men
have been transformed by the wiles of the enchantress.
On the morning of a beautiful day they reach a large
river, later found to be the Tiber, up which they sail for
a short distance amid the songs of many birds, finally
landing opposite Laurentum. vVergil then calls upon the
Muse Frato to describe the condition of Latium at the
time of Aeneas’ arrival. The king of the country is
Latinus, a child of the god Faunus. Latinus has no liv-
ing son; his daughter Lavinia has been wooed by many
suitors, of whom the most prominent is Turnus, the fiery
young king of the Rutuli. His pretensions to her daugh-
ter’s hand are favored by Queen Amata, but Latinus is
disturbed by portents, which indicate that Lavinia is
destined to be the bride of a foreigner. He consults the
oracle of Faunus, according to the prescribed ritual, which
confirms the portents.

The Trojans, after disembarking from their ships, pre-
pare and eat a simple meal. Having finished it, they
also eat the broad, flat wheaten loaves on which the viands
had been served, thus happily fulfilling the prophecy that
they were to devour their tables in the land which was to
be their home, a prediction made by the harpy Celaeno
(3, 255 ff.), but here attributed to Anchises. Aeneas there-

226

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOKS VII TO XII 227

upon offers a sacrifice to many gods, and Jupiter sends a
favorable sign. On the following day the Trojans explore
the new land. Aeneas dispatches an embassy to Latinus,
to ask for peace and friendship; meanwhile he lays out and
fortifies a camp on the coast. The envoys find the sub-
jects of King Latinus engaged in military exercises on
the plain before their city. The king receives them in
the splendid palace of Picus, adorned with wooden statues
of the native gods and heroes — Italus, Sabinus, Janus,
and others — and hung with trophies of war. He recog-
nizes the newcomers as Trojans, inquires the reason for
their coming, and bids them welcome, saying that Dar-
danus came originally from Italy. Ilioneus, as spokesman
of the delegation, declares that the Trojans have come to
Latium purposely and not by chance. He claims descent
from Jupiter for his race, tells of their voyage and their
adventures, and declares that they were directed to Latium
by Apollo. He presents the king with gifts sent by
Aeneas and asks permission to settle in Latium. Latinus
is silent for a time and meditates on the prophecies that
have been made. It seems to him that the foreign son-
in-law has arrived, who is to found a race that will con-
quer the world. He asks that Aeneas may come to him
in person, saying that if he is the one whom fate calls, he
will give him his daughter in marriage.

The relentless Juno, meanwhile, on her way from Argos
to Carthage, sees Aeneas and his followers preparing to
settle in Latium. She bursts into an angry soliloquy,
similar in tone to that of the First Book (37-49), review-
ing her vain attempts to check the Trojans. She declares
that she will resort to Acheron, since heaven opposes her,
and delay the proposed marriage, since she cannot prevent
it. She then sends the hateful fury, Allecto, to sow dis-

228 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOKS VII TO XII

cord between the Latins and the Trojans. Allecto hast-
ens to Latium and plants a serpent in the bosom of Amata,
who is already angered at the coming of a rival of Turnus.
At first the queen appeals to Latinus to prevent the mar-
riage of her daughter to “the Phrygian pirate,” as she
calls Aeneas. She declares that Turnus is of foreign race,
if that is a requisite for Lavinia’s husband. As the ser-
pent’s venom begins to work, she is roused to frenzy and
rushes madly through the city. She calls upon the Latin
matrons to join her in an orgy, and she carries off her
daughter with her to the woods, singing the marriage song
of Lavinia and Turnus.

Alecto then assumes the form of an aged priestess of
Juno, hies herself to Ardea, and tries to incite Turnus to
maintain his rights. by arms. Turnus, awakened in the
dead of night, laughs at the supposed priestess; he tells
her that he knew of the coming of the Trojans, that old
age has ended her days of prophecy, and that she should
leave war and arms to men. Upon this Allecto takes on
her own frightful form and hurls a torch at Turnus. He
is at last aroused to wrath and a desire for war; he calls
the Rutuli to arms and plans to march against Latinus.
The Fury next flies to the Trojan camp, where Iulus was
hunting. She incites his dogs to attack a beautiful pet
stag belonging to Latinus’ chief herdsman. Iulus mor-
tally wounds the animal with an arrow; it takes refuge
with its master, who rouses the country-folk to make war
upon the strangers. Inspired by Allecto, they gather in
a rage and fall upon the Trojans, while the Fury from the
roof gives the loud shepherds’ call. Whnen the battle is
raging, the monster triumphantly reports to Juno what
she has accomplished and offers to stir up further dissen-
sion. But she is sent back to the Lower World, disap-

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOKS VII TO XIr 229

pearing down a chasm in the Vale of Ampsanctus, while
Juno herself takes charge.

The shepherds, Turnus, and the kinsmen of the frenzied
matrons now join in a demand for war against the stran-
gers. Latinus resists for a time, firm as a sea-beaten cliff;
at last, after predicting the result of the strife, he shuts
himself in his palace, lays aside the reins of power, and
lets matters take their course. War is then formally de-
clared against the Trojans, according to the Latin cere-
monial custom, and since Latinus was unwilling to do it
himself, Juno throws open the gates of Janus’ shrine.
Five great cities forge arms and prepare for war; the sig-
nal is given and the forces assemble. An invocation of
the Muses is followed by a catalogue of the leaders and
their forces, including a description of Turnus in his splen-
did armor, and of Camilla, the Volscian warrior-maid,
swift as the wind and admired by all beholders.

The war begins. The Latins and their allies muster
their forces. They send a deputation to Argyripa, a city
of Apulia, to ask the aid of Diomedes. In the meantime
Aeneas is filled with anxious forebodings. Wwhen he finally
sleeps, Father Tiber appears to him in a dream; he calms
the hero’s fears and assures him that the Trojans have at
last found a permanent home, promising the omen of the
white sow which Helenus had predicted (3, 389 ff.). He
recommends Aeneas to apply for help to Evander the
Arcadian, who has settled on the Palatine Hill, and to
whom the river-god will guide him. Aeneas must also
make every effort to propitiate Juno; he may honor Father
Tiber after his victory. Upon awakening, Aeneas offers
a prayer to Father Tiber, promising him eternal worship
if he succeeds in founding his city. He then prepares for
his voyage up the river. Before his departure the white

230 VERCIL’S AENEID.— BOOKS VII TO XII

sow is found and sacrificed to Juno. All through the night
the Tiber checks its current, so that Aeneas and his com-
panions row on without effort and by the next midday
have reached the city of Evander.

The Trojans find the aged Arcadian occupied with sac-
rifices to Hercules, in company with his son Pallas and
his subjects. Wwhen the ships come into sight, Pallas
hastens to meet them and inquires who the newcomers
are. Aeneas, holding out an olive branch, replies that
they are Trojans, foes of the Latins, and asks. for aid
against the common enemy. Pallas invites them to go
to his father’s house. Aeneas explains to Evander that,
although as Trojans and Creeks they represent hostile
races, they are nevertheless sprung from a common an-
cestor, Atlas, and are threatened by a common enemy;
therefore they ought to be friends. Evander replies that
he recognizes Aeneas; he recalls having met Anchises in
Arcadia and says that Aeneas reminds him of his father.
He accepts the proffered alliance and invites the Trojans
to take part in the sacrificial feast which he is celebrating.
After the banquet Evander explains that the ceremony
commemorates their deliverance from the fire-breathing
giant, Cacus, son of Vulcan, who was for many years the
scourge of the neighborhood, but was slain by Hercules
for stealing his cattle. Hercules in his wrath attacked
Cacus, who took to his cave. The hero forced an en-
trance, slew the fire-breathing monster, and recovered his
cattle. As evening draws near the banquet is renewed,
and the Salii, bands of youths and of old men, sing a
hymn in praise of the god, recounting his exploits, his
labors, and his many triumphs. Evander then takes
Aeneas to his city, on the way telling him the earlier his-
tory of Latium — the coming of Saturn, the golden age,

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOKS VII TO XII 231

and the gradual growth of the love of gain and of power.
He points out the spots which later became famous as
parts of the city of Rome — the asylum of Romulus, the
Lupercal, the Capitol, and the Janiculum. He then wel-
comes Aeneas to his simple home, where he invites him
to spend the night, saying that Hercules had not disdained
his hospitality. Aeneas gladly accepts his offer.

Meanwhile Venus, fearful of her son’s safety, prevails
upon Vulcan to make him a suit of divine armor; she re-
minds the Lord of Fire that she had never before asked
him to aid the Trojans, and that he had made armor for
Memnon and Achilles. Vulcan grants her request; he
rises early and goes to his forges at the Lipari Islands,
where his Cyclopes are busy making thunderbolts for
Jupiter, a chariot for Mars, and an aegis for Minerva.
At Vulcan’s command they drop everything and take up
work on the armor for Aeneas, beginning with a gigantic
sevenfold shield.

Evander also rises with the dawn. Going to Aeness,
who is already astir, he tells him that, although his own
realm is small and his own forces few, he can secure for
the Trojans the powerful alliance of the Etruscans of
Argylla, who are eager to make war upon Turnus, because
he has received their deposed tyrant, the cruel and arro-
gant Mesentius, scorner of the gods. They are equipped
for battle, but are hesitating because of an oracle, which
has bidden them put themselves under the lead of a for-
eign chieftain. Evander has been asked to accept the
command, but he feels that he is too old. He urges
Aeneas to undertake the task and proposes to send with
him his own son Pallas and two hundred Arcadian horse-
men. Just then a brilliant flash is seen, thunder and a
trumpet-blast are heard, arms gleam and clash in the sky.

232 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOKS VII TO XII

Aeneas recognizes the signs as coming from Venus, and
foresees the doom of Turnus and the carnage that awaits
the Laurentines. He offers sacrifice to Hercules, in which
Evander joins, and then sends back the ships with a part
of his forces to Ascanius at the camp, to report what has
been accomplished. The Trojans who remain are given
horses, and they prepare to go to the Etruscan camp, ac-
companied by Pallas. As they depart, Evander says fare-
well to them with tears; he recalls his own exploits at
Praeneste and wishes that he might go with them. He
prays that he may live to welcome his son home safe and
sound, or die at once if he is not destined to see Pallas
again alive.

The Trojans and Arcadians advance toward the city of
Caere, with Pallas in the centre, conspicuous for his beau-
tiful armor; they halt in a grove near the camp of Tar-
chon, the Etruscan leader. Venus now brings the new
armor to Aeneas; he beholds it with admiration, especially
the shield, which is ornamented with scenes from the future
history of Rome: the story of Romulus and Remus, the
saving of the Capitol by Camillus, Catiline suffering pun-
ishnment in Hades, Cato happy in Elysium, and finally the
battle of Actium and the triumph of Augustus. Aeneass,
unaware of the meaning of it all, lifts upon his shoulders
the fame and fortunes of his descendants.

Juno now sends Iris down from heaven, to notify Tur-
nus of the absence of Aeneas and urge him to attack the
Trojan camp. Turnus obeys the summons and the Ru-
tuli rush like a mighty river against the foe. The followers
of Aeneas, seeing the advancing host, unwillingly hold
themselves within their fortifications, as Aeneas had com-
manded. Turnus, after rushing about the walls like a
ravening wolf and vainly trying to force an entrance, pre-

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOKS vVII TO XII 233

pares to set fire to the enemy’s fleet. The ships are with-
out defenders, but they are saved by Cybele; when they
were building, she had prayed to Jupiter that her sacred
wood, which she had given to the Trojans for their ships,
might never suffer destruction, and the king of the gods
had promised immortality to such of the vessels as should
succeed-in reaching Italy. As Turnus was about to burn
the fleet, a flash was seen in the sky and an awful voice
from Mount Ida was heard, bidding the ships go free;
whereupon they were all changed into sea-nymphs and
plunged into the depths. The Rutuli were astounded, but
Turnus, undismayed, encouraged them by explaining the
portent as unfavorable to the enemy, since it cut off their
retreat. The Trojans, he says, have fulfilled their des-
tiny by reaching Italy and have nothing more to hope
for; he will take this new Troy in broad daylight by force
of arms, and not by a nightly stratagem such as that to
which the original Ilium fell victim. Messapus, with a
large force, then encircles the fortifications, and the
watchers pass the night in drinking and revelry.

The Trojans in their camp also spent a wakeful night,
preparing for the morrow’s attack. Nisus, guardian of a
gate, tells his devoted friend Euryalus (see 5, 294 ff.) that
he is resolved to make his way through the enemy:’s lines
to Pallanteum, to summon Aeneas. Euryalus begs to be
allowed to accompany his friend. Nisus tries to dissuade
him, but at last reluctantly consents. The two youths
approach the Trojan leaders, who are holding a council
and wondering how to reach Aeneas. Nisus tells them
their plan; Aletes warmly approves their design and offers
thanks to the gods for such heroes. Ascanius begs them
to recall his father, promising ample rewards to both
youths, and to Euryalus in particular his personal friend-

234 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOKS VII TO XII

ship. The young Trojan commends his aged mother to
Ascanius, in case he should not return, saying that he is
leaving without bidding her farewell because he cannot
endure the sight of her tears. Ascanius declares that she
shall be as dear to him as if she were his own mother.
The chiefs then make a present of arms to the young
heroes, who set out on their perilous errand, followed by
the prayers of the whole army. They pass through the
camp of the Rutuli and slay many of the enemy in their
sleep. Finally Nisus warns his companion that dawn is
approaching, and they depart laden with spoils, includ-
ing the helmet of Messapus. As they are leaving the
hostile camp they are surprised by a body of three hun-
dred horsemen from Lanuvium under Volcens; these sur-
round Ēuryalus, who is betrayed by the glitter of Messa-
pus’ helmet. Nisus makes good his escape, but returns
in search of his friend. Finding him in the hands of the
enemy, he hurls spears into their midst, killing two of
their number. Volcens, not knowing whence the weap-
ons come, is about to retaliate by slaying Euryalus, when
Nisus rushes up and vainly tries to turn the blow upon
himself. Euryalus is slain; Nisus rushes madly upon the
foe, kills Volcens, but himself falls, pierced with many
wounds.

The victors bear the body of Volcens to the camp of the
Rutuli and the night’s carnage is revealed. At daybreak
the heads of Nisus and Euryalus are set up on poles, and
are recognized by their countrymen. The Trojans are
filled with sorrow and the mother of Euryalus, in a frengy
of grief, prays either that the enemy may slay her or that
Jupiter will strike her dead. The Volscians then attempt
to carry the Trojan camp by assault, advancing under a
tortoise-formation of shields, which the Trojans break up

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOKS VII TO XII 235

by hurling down huge stones. Under the lead of Turnus
the allies then attack and fire a lofty tower, which falls
in ruin upon its defenders. General carnage follows.

In the midst of the combat Numanus, Turnus’ brother-
in-law, reviles the Trojans and taunts them with effem-
inacy. This Ascanius could not endure, but after invok-
ing the aid of Jupiter, he for the first time aimed a shaft
at a human target and shot down the boaster. Apollo
applauded the act; then, coming to Ascanius in the guise
of the armor-bearer Butes, he advises the young man to
be content with that exploit and to attempt nothing more.
The gigantic brothers, Pandarus and Bitias, rashly throw
open the gate which they are guarding, and challenge the
foe to attempt an entrance. As they sally forth more
and more boldly, Turnus hurries to the spot, slays many
Trojans, and finally strikes down Bitias. Pandarus hast-
ily closes the gate, shutting out many of his countrymen
and shutting Turnus in among the Trojans. After per-
forming prodigies of valor, the Rutulian leaps into the
river and swims to his own men.

Meanwhile, on Mount Olympus, Jupiter has called a
council of the gods and is inquiring into the cause of the
strife. He declares that he had prohibited warfare be-
tween Troy and Italy; the time for war will come when
Carthage threatens Rome. Venus makes a plea for her
chosen people, praying that if it is against Jove’s will that
the Trojans have reached Italy, Ascanius at least may be
allowed to survive and dwell in Cyprus or Cythera. Juno
makes ar. indignant reply, declaring that the Trojans are
the aggressors and asking why she should not aid the Ru-
tuli if Venus helps her people. She advises Venus not to
meddle with arms, reminding her that it was through her
arts that Paris caused the Trojan war. A scene of confu-

236 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOKS VII TO XII

sion follows, which is quieted by Jupiter, who promises
not to interfere, but to leave each nation to fulfil its own
destiny. This he swore by the Styx and shook Olympus
with his nod.

At the Trojan camp the battle continued throughout
the day. Aeneas meanwhile sets out on his return, having
formed an alliance with the Etruscans, of whom a full cata-
logue is given, with their standards and their devices. On
his way he is met by the sea-nymphs into which his ships
had been changed. One of their number urges him to
hasten to his camp; she foretells his victory and Aeneas
prays to Cybele to confirm the omen. Reaching his camp
in the morning, Aeneas prepares for battle, signals to his
companions in the camp, and attempts a landing. The
beleaguered Trojans take heart, while the Rutuli are terri-
fied by the sheen of Aeneas’ helmet, which is like the
gleam of a comet or of the dog-star Sirius. Turnus, how-
ever, is undismayed and cheers his men on. Aeneas and
Tarchon succeed in getting ashore, although the Etrus-
can’s ship runs on a rock and is wrecked. A fierce battle
follows on the shore; the Arcadians begin to give way,
but Pallas rallies them and the Trojans come to their aid.
Pallas slays many of the Rutuli. He finally begins a sin-
gle combat with Lausus, son of Mexentius, but they were
not destined to finish their duel; for Turnus, advised by
Juturna of Lausus’ peril, attacks Pallas. The young Ar-
cadian invokes the aid of Hercules, who is unable to
oppose the power of fate; Pallas is slain by Turnus, who
restores his body to the Arcadians, but keeps the young
hero’s belt, an act which later proves his undoing.

Aeneas now appears on the field and slays many of the
Latin champions. Jupiter allows Juno to postpone Tur-
nus’ doom; she fashions a phantom in the image of

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOKS VII TO XII 237

Aeneas, which flees before Turnus. He follows it on
board a ship moored near the shore; Juno cuts the cable
and Turnus is carried away to Ardea, bewailing his fate.
Mexzentius now comes to the front, great as Orion and in
magic armor, and Aeneas goes to meet him. In the com-
bat which follows the Ētruscan is wounded and partly
disabled, but Lausus saves his father’s life at the cost of
his own. Aeneas pities the young man and restores his
body with its armor to his countrymen. Mexzentius, who
is resting by the river bank and washing his wounds, is
filled with grief as he sees his son’s body carried by. He
embraces the corpse, admits his past guilt, and determines
to die. Calling for his favorite horse, Rhoebus, he appeals
to the animal either to help him avenge his son or die with
him. Mezentius then challenges Aeneas, who gladly goes
to meet him. The Trojan wounds the horse, which falls
upon its rider. The Etruscan meets death unflinchingly.
asking to be protected from the vengeance of his subjects
and allowed to rest in the same tomb with his son Lausus.

At dawn on the next day Aeneas pays his vow to the
Lord of war and erects a trophy commemorating his vic-
tory over Mergentius. He bids his followers pay the last
rites to the dead, and sends the body of Pallas to Evander.
He returns to the place where the Arcadians and Trojans
are mourning over Pallas and addresses the dead hero.
The body is then wrapped in robes of gold and Ty9rian
purple and taken to Pallanteum, followed by the spoils
won by Pallas, prisoners to be sacrificed at his tomb, and
an escort of a thousand men. The Latins then ask for a
truce in order to bury their dead. Aeneas grants the re-
quest and proposes to put an end to the bloodshed by
staking everything on the issue of a duel with Turnus.
The aged Drances, a personal enemy of the Rutulian king,

238 VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOKS VII TO XII

thanks Aeneas and promises, if possible, to have the treaty
between the Latins and Trojans renewed. A truce of
twelve days follows.

Meanwhile rumor has brought the news of Pallas’
death to Evander, who comes out to meet the funeral
procession. He receives the body with bitter grief, but
glories in his son’s prowess, and in the thought that he
fell leading the Trojans into Latium. He begs Aeneas to
wreak vengeance upon Turnus. The Trojans and Latins
perform their funeral rites with due solemnity, including
a ceremonial running of armed men around the pyre and
the offering of gifts. The Latian women chide Turnus;
they call upon him to fight his own battles, and Drances
supports them in their demand. They hold a council,
during which Drances stirs up hostility against Turnus;
but many of those present side with the Rutulian. The
embassy which had been sent to Diomedes now returns
unsuccessful. The Greek hero warns the Latins to profit
by his experience and that of the other Greeks. Ulysses,
Menelaus, Agamemnon, himself, and many others have
paid severe penalties for their attack upon Troy. He ad-
vises peace rather than war; he extols the valor of Aeneas,
saying that with Hector he was the bravest of the defend-
ers of Troy. Latinus thereupon declares that the Latins
are waging an impious war; he proposes to give the Tro-
jans a part of his kingdom, or to fit out a fleet for them
in case they wish to go elsewhere. He recommends send-
ing them a delegation with gifts and other tokens of
friendship. Drances, still hostile and jealous of Turnus,
reviles the Rutulian king and advises Latinus to give his
daughter in marriage to Aeneas; he calls upon Turnus
either to consent to peace or to engage Aeneas in single
combat. Turnus retorts by calling upon Drances him-

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOKS VII TO XII 239

self to enter the lists; he boasts of his former victories and
urges Latinus not to give up hope. At last, however, he
agrees to meet Aeneas.

While they are thus debating, a messenger reports that
the Trojan king is advancing upon the city; the Latins
rush to arms. Amata and the Latin matrons, accompa-
nied by Lavinia, go to the temple of Pallas, where the
matrons pray for the defeat of “the Phrygian pirate.”
Turnus arms and sallies forth; at the. gate he is met by
the Volscian heroine, Camilla, who offers to engage the
Etruscan cavalry with her own forces. Turnus, however,
bids her join with him and lays an ambuscade for the
Trojans in a deep vale, approached by a narrow path.
Diana now sends her attendant, Opis, to avenge the im-
pending death of Camilla; she tells Opis the story of the
maiden’s birth, of her separation from her mother, and
of her bringing up in the wilderness by her father. A
battle ensues between the horsemen of the two armies,
in which Camilla distinguishes herself by slaying many
Trojan champions. At last Orsilochus challenges her to
meet him on foot and is killed.

Jupiter then rouses the Etruscan leader Tarchon to
wrath, and he takes the field. He captures Venulus alive,
encourages the Tuscans, and for a time turns the tide of
battle. Meanwhile Aruns is craftily following Camilla,
watching for a favorable moment to attack her. This
comes as she is recklessly pursuing one of the Trojans,
eager to possess his rich armor; Aruns hurls a spear at
her, praying to Apollo that he may slay the warrior-maid
and return home in triumph. Apollo grants half his
prayer; Camilla falls, but Aruns is slain with an arrow
by Opis and left forgotten on the plain. At last the
Rutuli are routed; Aeneas advances upon the city, but

240 VERCIL’S AENEID.— BOOKS VII TO XIT

before he reaches it, night falls. The citizens prepare for
defense and even the women arm.

Whnen Turnus sees that the Latins are faint-hearted be-
cause of their reverses his spirits rise. He asks Latinus
to call a truce, declaring that he is ready for the duel
with Aeneas. Latinus tries to dissuade him from meet-
ing the Trojan hero, advising him rather to give up La-
vinia and consent to a peace. Amata also vainly appeals
to Turnus, who arms for the combat. Both sides enter
into a solemn compact to abide by the issue of the duel.
Juno, who is viewing the scene from the Alban Mount,
addresses Turnus’ sister, the nymph Juturna, and begs
her to prevent the duel. The Latins and Trojans meet
before the walls. Aeneas declares that he will withdraw
to the city of Evander if he is defeated; if victorious, he
will unite on equal terms with the Latins. Latinus also
promises eternal peace, whatever the issue.

The Rutuli consider the contest an unequal one, and
in addition to their sympathy for Turnus they are influ-
enced by an unfavorable omen. Tolumnius therefore
hurls a spear into the midst of the Trojans, killing one
of their number, and a general conflict follows. Aeneas,
while trying to restore order, is wounded by an arrow and
leaves the field. Turnus takes advantage of his absence
to deal havoc among the Trojans. After the physician
Iapyx has vainly tried his skill, Aeneas is healed by Venus;
he embraces Ascanius and returns to the fray. He seeks
everywhere for Turnus, but Juturna, who has assumed
the form of her brother’s charioteer, Metiscus, keeps him
out of Aeneas’ reach. Meanwthile, in different parts of
the field, Aeneas and Turnus slay many adversaries;
finally the Trojan hero, at the suggestion of Venus, ad-
vances on the city, reproaching Latinus for what has

VERGIL’S AENEID.— BOOKS VII TO XII 241

happened and calling the gods to witness that he has been
forced to do battle.

Saces, sorely wounded, then approaches Turnus and
appeals to him to defend the Latin capital. The Ru-
tulian resolves to encounter Aeneas, who stays his attack
on the town. Thne duel begins; Juno makes a last appeal
to Jupiter, and, while she does not succeed in saving Tur-
nus, she exacts a promise that the Trojans shall not im-
pose their name upon the Latins. Juturna, in the guise
of Metiscus, renders aid to Turnus, whereupon Venus
helps Aeneas. Finally Jupiter sends a Fury to call Ju-
turna from the field; Turnus is defeated and begs for his
life. Aeneas is on the point of granting his prayer when
he catches sight of the belt of Pallas on the body of his
antagonist and slays him.
